    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }

<div id="descriptionContainer">
  
      <div id="description" class="readable stacked" style="right:0">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer12650340630285501454"><i>Wide Sargasso Sea</i>, a masterpiece of modern fiction, was Jean Rhys’s return to the literary center stage. She had a startling early career and was known for her extraordinary prose and haunting women characters. With <i>Wide Sargasso Sea</i>, her last and best-selling novel, she ingeniously brings into light one of fiction’s most fascinating characters: the madwoman in the attic f</span>
  <span id="freeText12650340630285501454" style="display:none"><i>Wide Sargasso Sea</i>, a masterpiece of modern fiction, was Jean Rhys’s return to the literary center stage. She had a startling early career and was known for her extraordinary prose and haunting women characters. With <i>Wide Sargasso Sea</i>, her last and best-selling novel, she ingeniously brings into light one of fiction’s most fascinating characters: the madwoman in the attic from Charlotte Brontë’s <i>Jane Eyre</i>. This mesmerizing work introduces us to Antoinette Cosway, a sensual and protected young woman who is sold into marriage to the prideful Mr. Rochester. Rhys portrays Cosway amidst a society so driven by hatred, so skewed in its sexual relations, that it can literally drive a woman out of her mind.<br /><br />A new introduction by the award-winning Edwidge Danticat, author most recently of <i>Claire of the Sea Light</i>, expresses the enduring importance of this work. Drawing on her own Caribbean background, she illuminates the setting’s impact on Rhys and her astonishing work.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12650340630285501454" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

      </div>

          $$('div.prototip').each(function(i){i.setStyle({zIndex: $('box').getStyle('z-index')})});
        } else {
          $$('div.prototip').each(function(i){i.setStyle({zIndex: 6000})});
        }
      });

      newTip['wrapper'].addClassName('prototipAllowOverflow');

        $('edition_829372').observe('prototip:shown', function () {
          $$('div.prototip').each(function (e) {
            if ($('edition_829372').hasClassName('ignored')) {
              e.setStyle({'display': 'none'});
              return;
            }
            e.setStyle({'overflow': 'visible'});
          });
        });
      $('edition_829372').observe('prototip:hidden', function () {
        $$('span.elementTwo').each(function (e) {
          if (e.getStyle('display') !== 'none') {
            var lessLink = e.next();
            swapContent(lessLink);
          }
        });
      });

Popular Answered Questions

<div id='communityQuestionsWithAnswers'>
<div class='communityQuestionAndAnswer'>
<div class='communityQuestion'>
<div class='questionText'>
<a href="/questions/505440-is-this-a-plausible-portrayal-of">Is this a plausible portrayal of Rochester, or is he (however little) twisted in order to fit the message of the book?</a>
</div>
<div class='communityQAFooter questionFooter'>
<ul class='questionFooterActions'>
<li class='footerItem like'>
<span class='likeItContainer' id='like_container_community_question_505440'><a id="like_count_community_question_505440" rel="nofollow" href="/rating/voters/505440?resource_type=CommunityQuestion"><span class="likesCount">22 likes</span></a>&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;<a rel="nofollow" class="jsLike like_it" href="https://www.goodreads.com/user/new?remember=true&amp;return_url=%2Fbook%2Fshow%2F25622780-wide-sargasso-sea">like</a></span>

</li>
<li class='footerItem communityQuestionTimestamp'>
<a href="/questions/505440-is-this-a-plausible-portrayal-of">5 years ago</a>
</li>
<li class='footerItem communityQuestionPermalink'>
<a href="/questions/505440-is-this-a-plausible-portrayal-of">See all 14 answers</a>
</li>
</ul>
</div>

</div>
<div class='allCommunityAnswers'>
<div class='communityAnswer'>
<div class='communityAnswerBody'>
<div class='answererUserIcon'>
<a class="leftAlignedImage userPhoto" href="/user/show/27518476-dani-lle-wierenga"><img alt="" src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/users/1464293807i/27518476._UX100_CR0,0,100,100_.jpg" /></a>
</div>
<div class='communityAnswerTextContainer'>
<span class='communityAnswererName'>
<a href="/user/show/27518476-dani-lle-wierenga">Daniëlle Wierenga</a>
</span>
<span class='communityAnswerText'>
<span class="expandableContainer"><span class="truncatedContent">I think the most important way to think about this novel is in a post-colonial context. Rochester is a British man, Antoinette is a creole Caribbean w<a class="jsShow showAction" href="#">…more</a></span><span class="fullContent">I think the most important way to think about this novel is in a post-colonial context. Rochester is a British man, Antoinette is a creole Caribbean woman (White, yes, but not accepted by other Europeans). Rochester lusts for Antoinette, but also feels he must control her. He hates the Caribbean and doesn't want to be there. By controlling Antoinette, he controls this foreign place, just as the British controlled and ruled over their colonies. <br /><br />People who grew up in these colonies, like Jean Rhys, know that colonial rule was not the golden age for them that it was for the Europeans who gained so much wealth. It was brutal, abusive, and exploitative. It was also exemplified by a licentious greed and a need to consume and control. That doesn't mean all colonies hate the powers that once ruled over them and see them as evil. It's a complicated relationship, like that of Rochester &amp; Antoinette. He wants to make her an English woman, but she could never be that. It drives her mad. No matter how hard the British tried, none of their colonies became the copies of themselves that they wanted.<br /><br />It's totally possible that the young Rochester of Rhys's novel and the old Rochester of Jane Eyre are the same person. He did something horrible, something he knew was horrible, to Antoinette. He now has to live with the consequences. Maybe he learned. Maybe he didn't. In England it doesn't matter. That's his comfort zone. When he's in it, he's charming and brooding. When he's out of it, he's manipulative and unstable. I think that people upset about this duality have romanticized Rochester too much. <a class="jsHide hideAction" href="#">(less)</a></span></span>
</span>
</div>
</div>
<div class='communityAnswerFooter'>

<span class='communityAnswerText'>
<span class="expandableContainer"><span class="truncatedContent">No, you should read Jane Eyre first. Even though you may enjoy this book without understanding the history of Jane Eyre, you will not grasp the depth <a class="jsShow showAction" href="#">…more</a></span><span class="fullContent">No, you should read Jane Eyre first. Even though you may enjoy this book without understanding the history of Jane Eyre, you will not grasp the depth of emotions expressed without understanding what happened in Jane Eyre. <a class="jsHide hideAction" href="#">(less)</a></span></span>

<div id="review_1807279658" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1807279658" />
    <a title="Sean Barrs " class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/27788046-sean-barrs"><img alt="Sean Barrs " src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1605281953p2/27788046.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1807279658?book_show_action=true">Nov 09, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sean Barrs " class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sean Barrs " href="/user/show/27788046-sean-barrs">Sean Barrs </a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780141182858" href="/book/show/829372.Wide_Sargasso_Sea">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">those who want the full picture</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/27788046-sean-barrs?shelf=postcolonial">postcolonial</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/27788046-sean-barrs?shelf=darkness-horror-gothic">darkness-horror-gothic</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/27788046-sean-barrs?shelf=love-and-romance">love-and-romance</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/27788046-sean-barrs?shelf=5-star-reads">5-star-reads</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1807279658" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10842130153077355592">Bertha Mason is the madwoman in the attic; she is the raving lunatic that is Rochester’s first wife in <i>Jane Eyre,</i>but have you ever stopped to wonder what her side of the story is? Have you ever considered that she may have a tale to tell? <br/><br/>Jean Rhys has, and she tells it to you in all its traumatic colours. Our crazy lunatic isn’t that far from Jane. Bronte describes her as a semi-human, an animal that growls and raves as she stalks the hall of Thornfield like some unidentifiable spectre. But wha</span>
  <span id="freeText10842130153077355592" style="display:none">Bertha Mason is the madwoman in the attic; she is the raving lunatic that is Rochester’s first wife in <i>Jane Eyre,</i>but have you ever stopped to wonder what her side of the story is? Have you ever considered that she may have a tale to tell? <br><br>Jean Rhys has, and she tells it to you in all its traumatic colours. Our crazy lunatic isn’t that far from Jane. Bronte describes her as a semi-human, an animal that growls and raves as she stalks the hall of Thornfield like some unidentifiable spectre. But what drove her to this state? What made her this way? Well the simple answer is a man named Rochester. As the second son of a rich family, he needed a means of creating his own wealth. What's the answer to his problem? Marry some rich girl and steal all her money and not worry about the consequences, but there more to it than this. Do you remember that scene in <i> Jane Eyre</i> where Rochester tries to dominate Jane and make her into something else by picking out her clothes? Perhaps Bertha had this but on a more intense scale. <br><br>Indeed, Bertha isn’t even her real name. Rhys names the character Antoinette, a name Rochester refuses to use when he learns of her past. Antoinette has a family history of insanity on the maternal side, but, again there is more to it than this. What creates this insanity? For Antoinette it is the simple of act of belonging nowhere. She is a hybrid, a figure that walks between cultures. As a white European girl she was raised in Jamaica; thus, she is neither fully Jamaican nor European. This sounds very similar to the role of the governess, a figure that belonged to no particular class structure. Neither culture would accept Antoinette as one of their own, as she herself recognises: <br><br><i>“It was a song about a white cockroach. That’s me. That’s what they call all of us who were here before their own people in Africa sold them to the slave traders. And I’ve heard English woman call us white niggers. So between you I often wonder who I am and where is my country and where do I belong and why was I ever born at all.” </i> <br><br> <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1479650009i/21219213._SY540_.jpg" width="400" height="500" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"> <br><br>She is stuck in-between with an uncertain identity, so when this Rochester figure comes along proposing love she is swept away. Could she really be this happy? This man offers her hope and a new life, but it is all a lie. When she finds out it breaks her. The last bastion of refuge shatters and she realises her hate for this false man: she finds yet another place she doesn’t belong. Rochester takes his grief stricken wife home, and shoves her in an attic. Then BOOM! He finds himself utterly shocked at the manifestation of her madness. Such a fool.<br><br>We cannot blame Bronte for her depiction of Bertha. Bronte wrote during the peak of the British Empire; these ideas were imbedded into her cultural psyche: this is how the Victorians saw the world. Bronte was unconsciously aware of this; she even went as far as to apologise at a later date for her depiction of Bertha. She didn’t fully consider how it would be received. In her fixation with women’s rights in an unjust Western society, she failed to look beyond the realms of the English experience. But that is not to overlook the phenomenal achievements of <i> Jane Eyre.</i> It does wonders for recognising the voice of women; however, Jean Rhys just goes a little bit further.<br><br><a href="https://www.facebook.com/sean.barrs" rel="nofollow noopener">Facebook</a>| <a href="https://twitter.com/ultra_vegan" rel="nofollow noopener">Twitter</a>| <a href="https://www.instagram.com/sean.ultra_vegan/?hl=en" rel="nofollow noopener">Insta</a>| <a href="https://york.academia.edu/SeanBarrs" rel="nofollow noopener">Academia</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="10842130153077355592" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer12406602787163001185">In short - incoherent overpraised rubbish. <br/><br/>I have read my share of classics over the years. Some of them were boring, some outside the area of my interest, but never had I come across one that was so dreadfully bad and at the same time so critically acclaimed.<br/><br/>I simply can't comprehend how this jumble of disjointed sentences can be seriously called a "masterpiece." The story was almost impossible to follow. Had I not read "Jane Eyre," I'd be lost in this book completely. The characters' motivatio</span>
  <span id="freeText12406602787163001185" style="display:none">In short - incoherent overpraised rubbish. <br /><br />I have read my share of classics over the years. Some of them were boring, some outside the area of my interest, but never had I come across one that was so dreadfully bad and at the same time so critically acclaimed.<br /><br />I simply can't comprehend how this jumble of disjointed sentences can be seriously called a "masterpiece." The story was almost impossible to follow. Had I not read "Jane Eyre," I'd be lost in this book completely. The characters' motivations and even actions were hard to understand, their personalities were non-existent. And apparently, Bertha went mad because Rochester didn't give her enough loving and cheated on her with a servant girl once. <br /><br />Awful beyond belief.<br /><br />Reading challenge: #27, 1 of 2. </span>

<div id="review_395355906" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/395355906" />
    <a title="Emily May" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4622890-emily-may"><img alt="Emily May" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1528390751p2/4622890.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/395355906?book_show_action=true">Aug 19, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Emily May" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Emily May" href="/user/show/4622890-emily-may">Emily May</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780241281901" href="/book/show/29429901-wide-sargasso-sea">review of another edition</a>


          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Emily May by: </span>
      1001 Books You Must Read Before You Die 
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4622890-emily-may?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4622890-emily-may?shelf=2012">2012</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4622890-emily-may?shelf=clothbound-wishlist">clothbound-wishlist</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer395355906" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4216663111457900540">Beware of a few Jane Eyre spoilers if you've managed to live your life so far without a) reading it, or b) knowing what happens.<br/>..............................................................................................................................<br/><br/>One thing that really gets on my nerves is when an author writes a book about another author's story/character/whatever and you cannot understand or appreciate what you are being given unless you read the first author's work. Now, I have read <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/10210.Jane_Eyre' title='Jane Eyre by Charlotte Brontë' rel='nofollow noopener'>Jan</a></span>
  <span id="freeText4216663111457900540" style="display:none">Beware of a few Jane Eyre spoilers if you've managed to live your life so far without a) reading it, or b) knowing what happens.<br />..............................................................................................................................<br /><br />One thing that really gets on my nerves is when an author writes a book about another author's story/character/whatever and you cannot understand or appreciate what you are being given unless you read the first author's work. Now, I have read <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/10210.Jane_Eyre" title="Jane Eyre by Charlotte Brontë" rel="nofollow noopener">Jane Eyre</a> many times, but If I hadn't I would have been clueless as to what Rhys was babbling on about here. For me, this book really demonstrates that the <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/452208.1001_Books_You_Must_Read_Before_You_Die" title="1001 Books You Must Read Before You Die by Peter Boxall" rel="nofollow noopener">1001 Books You Must Read Before You Die</a> list really does not mean "The 1001 Best Books Ever". <br /><br />And I do appreciate the original idea behind <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/25022.Jean_Rhys" title="Jean Rhys" rel="nofollow noopener">Jean Rhys</a>' novel. The mad woman in Mr Rochester's attic had a story to tell, it has long bothered feminists and other critics how this character was portrayed in <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/10210.Jane_Eyre" title="Jane Eyre by Charlotte Brontë" rel="nofollow noopener">Jane Eyre</a> because, at the end of the day, this mad woman was a person with a history - or should have been - not just a little crazy puppet there to pop up and throw a spanner in the works when Jane and Mr Rochester finally got together. Rhys wanted to give her the past that Bronte didn't, and she also wanted to show her decline into madness so the reader could appreciate who she was and where she came from and why she ended up the way she did.<br /><br />I just don't think it was handled very well and I didn't like the writing style at all. The narrative relies upon dream-like visions, fragmented impressions, incomplete sentences, and multiple first-person voices to create an overall sense of disorientation in the reader... or so is the intention according to my little bit of google research. I'd say "complete bewilderment" is more accurate than "disorientation". I find that I can't appreciate this feeling of being drugged up to my eyeballs when reading a book, though I know many readers look on it favourably. It's more trippy than beautiful to me.<br /><br />Plus, I think the attempts to show how she became mad were a failure. This book appears on lists like "Novels for Feminists" and "100 Books Every Woman Should Read" - why? When this is a book about a woman who falls into madness because she distrusts her husband and their relationship is falling apart. I appreciate that it isn't feminism if the woman is always strong and never makes mistakes, but she basically crumbles because her husband doesn't give her enough attention. Not very believable, and not very pro-woman either.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4216663111457900540" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_2726473402" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2726473402" />
    <a title="Violet wells" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4196681-violet-wells"><img alt="Violet wells" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442260743p2/4196681.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2726473402?book_show_action=true">Mar 05, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Violet wells" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Violet wells" href="/user/show/4196681-violet-wells">Violet wells</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2726473402" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10094173750244323986">I've always been convinced I've read Jane Eyre. I've even rated it here. I also thought I had at some point in my life seen a film adaptation. But the further I ventured into this retelling of Charlotte Bronte's novel the more I found myself doubting the veracity of this assumption. Finally, I had to own up to never having read Jane Eyre. This came as a bit of a shock, as it always does when we discover we have invented a memory. No doubt I once fibbed, not wanting to embarrass myself as being p</span>
  <span id="freeText10094173750244323986" style="display:none">I've always been convinced I've read Jane Eyre. I've even rated it here. I also thought I had at some point in my life seen a film adaptation. But the further I ventured into this retelling of Charlotte Bronte's novel the more I found myself doubting the veracity of this assumption. Finally, I had to own up to never having read Jane Eyre. This came as a bit of a shock, as it always does when we discover we have invented a memory. No doubt I once fibbed, not wanting to embarrass myself as being poorly read and the fib became a monument, a monument I've even garnished with (four-starred) flowers. <br /><br />And if you've never read Jane Eyre this novel is sometimes confusing. It may even be confused. It begins with Antoinette, a young Creole girl whose family on her white father's side have a dubious history of slave-holding. Slavery has recently been abolished and there's much anger in the air. The racism theme in this novel is for the most part artfully dramatised, especially perhaps the close bond between racism and misogyny. As if you can't have one without the other. (Women too can be misogynistic, if they've been browbeaten and brainwashed .) Part one, culminating with the eruption of the violence simmering throughout, is all beautifully observed and compelling. <br /><br />Then we begin part two and I found myself reading three pages which were going completely over my head. I retraced my footsteps and found a footnote. The note at the back of the book informed me the book was now being narrated by Antoinette's new husband. Should a novel need this kind of note? I recently watched a documentary about Rhys' editor and learned that the first part of this book was added afterwards at her suggestion. Which means Rhys composed the novel in a completely different key. And I'm afraid this shows. I kept feeling Rhys didn't have full control over her material. As if the new beginning had skewed and jarred what followed. To my mind, the editor should have gone further and told her to now write the entire novel from Antoinette's point of view. The husband narrative, never entirely convincing - what baddie portrays himself as a baddie? - might have been interesting had Rhys sought to undermine Antoinette's truth with his truth - created a battle of two unreliable narrators. But he's a reliable narrator, too reliable, there essentially to establish facts. It struck me as a laziness in Rhys that, having changed the beginning, she left what follows untouched. And all the best bits of the husband's narrative are when he sounds exactly like Antoinette. That Rhys eventually dumps him for part three and returns to Antoinette does make you wonder why he was ever there in the first place. (She also very clumsily adds a brief third voice in part three which confirmed to me that she gave far more thought to crafting her sentences than she did to structure.) It would have demanded a more refined artistry but my feeling was Rhys could have and should have channelled all the information we learn through the husband through Antoinette. <br /><br />What redeems the architectural flaws is how well Rhys writes and how much of interest she has to say about her subject matter. It's sad if this, as the cover proclaims, is her masterpiece because my feeling is she was more than capable of writing a better novel. The average rating for this is 3.58 and that's pretty much how I felt. And I haven't read this three times as this site declares. But I have now read it. Jane Eyre will follow soon.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10094173750244323986" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_258413424" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/258413424" />
    <a title="Petra-X" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1237196-petra-x"><img alt="Petra-X" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1603179398p2/1237196.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/258413424?book_show_action=true">Jan 08, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Petra-X" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Petra-X" href="/user/show/1237196-petra-x">Petra-X</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780393308808" href="/book/show/481558.Wide_Sargasso_Sea">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1237196-petra-x?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1237196-petra-x?shelf=2020-100-reviews">2020-100-reviews</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1237196-petra-x?shelf=reviewed">reviewed</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1237196-petra-x?shelf=books-read-a-long-time-ago">books-read-a-long-time-ago</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1237196-petra-x?shelf=caribbean">caribbean</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1237196-petra-x?shelf=getting-a-bit-personal-here">getting-a-bit-personal-here</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer258413424" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17638316922439293515">I read this book years ago and loved it as much as <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/10210.Jane_Eyre' title='Jane Eyre by Charlotte Brontë' rel='nofollow noopener'>Jane Eyre</a> of which this book was the prequel. I also wrote a review years ago but it not only disappeared, the edition was changed. How do these things happen? There is very little about the book in the following. But quite a bit about Dominica and Sargasso and a little of Jean Rhys herself.<br/><br/>Years ago I used to go to Dominica, I stayed in three places. Firstly was an old Great House in Roseau, the capital, Cherry Orchard which wasn't it's origina</span>
  <span id="freeText17638316922439293515" style="display:none">I read this book years ago and loved it as much as <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/10210.Jane_Eyre" title="Jane Eyre by Charlotte Brontë" rel="nofollow noopener">Jane Eyre</a> of which this book was the prequel. I also wrote a review years ago but it not only disappeared, the edition was changed. How do these things happen? There is very little about the book in the following. But quite a bit about Dominica and Sargasso and a little of Jean Rhys herself.<br /><br />Years ago I used to go to Dominica, I stayed in three places. Firstly was an old Great House in Roseau, the capital, Cherry Orchard which wasn't it's original name and was at one time the home of Jean Rhys. There were nuns staying there too. And the largest horriblest millipede I have ever seen, in the toilet. It wasn't dead, it reared it's head up at me. It was then I decided to leave, and bought The Wide Sargasso Sea.<br /><br />I went to Laudat as I was into climbing, hill-walking really, we only used ropes once. That was because the rasta whose guest house I was staying in, One Love, wanted to go to Roseau shopping and it was a long hike on the road but there was a short cut if you went down the mountain, that needed ropes. I didn't know what I was letting myself in for, this was not the sort of life I was used to! So I hitched a ride back. (Very uncomfortable, standing up in the back of a pickup bouncing at high speed over a pot-holed 'road' and had to pay the driver in US$ as well).<br /><br />When I was in Laudat I had a couple of amazing experiences. I trekked to the <a href="http://images-resrc.staticlp.com/S=W1000M,H700M/O=85/http://media.lonelyplanet.com/a/g/hi/t/1ad6750a3e6f91617c64ded785e4f7ed-boiling-lake.jpg" rel="nofollow noopener">the Boiling Lake</a> which is a long,hard climb - you can tell when you are getting closer because the air stinks of sulphur. I also went to the <a href="https://external-content.duckduckgo.com/iu/?u=http%3A%2F%2Fdominicanewsonline.com%2Fnews%2Fwp-content%2Fuploads%2F2011%2F04%2Ffresh-water-lake-1.jpg&amp;f=1&amp;nofb=1" rel="nofollow noopener">Freshwater Lake</a> which was peaceful and apparently sacred to the Carib and Arawak Indians. <br /><br />When I was there the rasta said he wanted to eat some fresh meat so I thought we were going back to Roseau but no, he strung up a goat into a small tree by it's hind legs and sawed at its throat, the poor creature screaming and blood everywhere (I had headphones on, Michael Jackson, but I could still hear it). Then he sliced open its belly and brought out the liver and said he would cook it first. His sister fried it with local herbs, it smelled horrible, old goat and I could not bring myself to even put any on my plate. I stuck to ground provisions, yam, dasheen, yucca and Irish potatoes. Next day I left.<br /><br />I went to stay in the Carib Reserve with the bwoyfrien's family. He was half Carib <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ and half the son of one of the Green's who grandfather had been an engineer on the Panama Canal who had settled in Dominica. His son had children by a lot of women, Black, White and Carib, he could afford it! But all the children loved him and got a university education if they wanted it. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> At that time there were no guest houses, nothing at all. I stayed in a simple wooden house. The first night I had salt fish with roast breadfruit cooked on an open fire. It was amazing. <br /><br />Next day we picked cocoa pods and threw the pink beans on a big mat on the ground to dry in the sun and ferment. The cocoa beans were surrounded by white 'cottonwool' which was delicious and juicy and lemony, a real surprise. Every day we raked the beans until they were brown, then they were ground up and formed into hard balls mixed with cinnamon and nutmeg to grate for hot chocolate. At night I read the book by the light of an oil lamp.<br /><br />I could relate to the book from Jean Rhys's life. Antoinette was neither really Jamaican or European. Jean was in a similar situation. She didn't get on with her mother, her father was harsh, she spoke Kweyol,tthe local French patois as well as English, but was not really accepted by the Blacks. Sent to the UK for schooling at 16, she didn't fit in there any better and was mocked for her accent and less than standard English. She wasn't rich, destined to be a debutante, but was expected to return to the island and marry another white Dominican, which she didn't want to do. Nowhere did she fit in, and that is Antoinette too. Antoinette went mad, and Jean became bad - a chorus girl, a prostitute, a kept woman and then..... a brilliant author.<br /><br />The wide Sargasso Sea seems to be expanding. Sargasso is an orange algae, a seaweed that floats in long drifting lines you can see from the plane but ends up in <a href="https://www.google.com/search?q=mats+of+orange+sargasso+in+marinas&amp;source=lnms&amp;tbm=isch&amp;sa=X&amp;ved=2ahUKEwj5hfzl78DsAhVQo1kKHYHMBS0Q_AUoAnoECBUQBA&amp;biw=1193&amp;bih=540&amp;dpr=1.15" rel="nofollow noopener">huge mats in marinas and beaches</a> on the South/East side of the islands. It can totally cover a beach and be as deep as up to your knees. And it stinks, it stinks worse than the Boiling Lake. Rotting vegetation, rotting eggs, it's really nasty and since 2011 every single year it has drifted all over the Caribbean stinking up the shoreline, no one knows why. It has to be dredged, raked and trucked away. Then all of a sudden, the season will be over and everything is ok, back to the clear waters and fresh breezes and we forget all about the stink of the sargasso. I wonder if Dominica had this awful plague back in Jean Rhys's time she'd have changed the title of her book?<br /><br /><i>Written 14th June 2020 since the original review and correct edition disappeared</i></span>
  <a data-text-id="17638316922439293515" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1610028455" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1610028455" />
    <a title="Elyse  Walters" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/597461-elyse-walters"><img alt="Elyse  Walters" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1514246949p2/597461.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1610028455?book_show_action=true">Jul 15, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Elyse  Walters" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Elyse  Walters" href="/user/show/597461-elyse-walters">Elyse  Walters</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1610028455" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16360628911498858115">“After Leaving Mr. Mackenzie”, was so terrific that I ordered two more books by Jean Rhys to read.<br/>“Good Morning, Midnight”, - will be next to read...<br/><br/>“Wide Sargasso Sea”, was Rhys most famous book.... quoted as a masterpiece...bringing the fascinating character- Antoinette Cosway- from Charlotte Brontë’s “Jane Eyre”, out of the dark attic...and putting her center stage. <br/>I agree - the entire concept for this book was brilliant- fascinating- and it worked. <br/>What an incredible risk, Rhys made!! Truly</span>
  <span id="freeText16360628911498858115" style="display:none">“After Leaving Mr. Mackenzie”, was so terrific that I ordered two more books by Jean Rhys to read.<br />“Good Morning, Midnight”, - will be next to read...<br /><br />“Wide Sargasso Sea”, was Rhys most famous book.... quoted as a masterpiece...bringing the fascinating character- Antoinette Cosway- from Charlotte Brontë’s “Jane Eyre”, out of the dark attic...and putting her center stage. <br />I agree - the entire concept for this book was brilliant- fascinating- and it worked. <br />What an incredible risk, Rhys made!! Truly was amazing. <br /><br />Funny... I had just recently read “Mona, in Three Act”, by Op de Beeck Griet... and my complaint was it was 400+ pages long. This book, half its length had twice as much lasting power. <br /><br />The introduction, by Edwidge Danticat put me in the right framework to plow ahead.<br />I became interested with what Danticat was fascinated with. <br />Edwidge Danticat had a Caribbean background, as Rhys did. <br />She wrote:<br />“The fact that Bertha Antoinette Mason—the first Mrs.Rochester—was a Creole, a white person, who was born and raised on a Caribbean island, fascinated me. I was sad, though, that we readers spent so little time with her, and that when we did see her she was either raging mad or setting things on fire. There must be something more, I told myself. How did Bertha Mason come to be confined in our old house in England? Was she madly in love or simply mad? Had Mr. Rochester once loved her the way he loves Jane Eyre?”<br /><br />And so the story begins with Antoinette Cosway narrating PART ONE.<br />I READ THE FIRST THREE SENTENCES SEVERAL TIMES.<br />“They say when trouble comes close ranks, and so the white people did. But we were not in their ranks”. The Jamaican ladies had never approved of my mother, ‘because she pretty like pretty self’, Christophine said”.<br />Complex race and gender relations revealed themselves right away.<br />This theme continues throughout.....( Antoinette is later called “white cockroach” and “white nigger”), along with themes of emotional repression, slaves &amp; slave owners, frustration and desperation, class, gender, love &amp; hate, sensuality, jealousy, abuse, feminism, possessiveness, mental illness, control, ( Mr. Rochester re-naming Antoinette, Bertha), madness, and a relationship between money, lust, sex, and power. <br /><br />Mr. Rochester marries Antoinette....partly for money ....but also in part to have power of her ....and partly for lust. It’s such a doomed marriage. Antoinette tries to ride out the racial tensions and family struggles.<br /><br />PART TWO is the longest section - narrated mostly by Mr. Rochester. He tries to turn Antoinette into something she isn’t. <br /><br />PART THREE.... the shortest section ... perhaps the saddest part of the entire book - but we see the parallels to PART ONE...<br /><br />The characters want freedom...characters were often drunk...the Caribbean was a dream for Mr. Rochester....<br />England a dream for Antoinette....<br />But DREAMS....can literally drive a person mad. <br /><br />Many great reviews already written....<br />I completely agree... this was a phenomenal book. <br />I read it slow - I re-read parts several times. I reflected on it during the times I wasn’t reading it often. It’s a slim book that I also plan to read again. <br />It’s simply haunting in my thoughts. <br />The female characters- Christophine, a former slave, and Antoinette, both will remain in my thoughts in much the same way Jane Eyre has. <br /><br />Loved it and agree with others...this was a phenomenal- tragic <br />novel. <br />Once again proving a thin novel of 200 pages can have more power than books twice its length. <br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="16360628911498858115" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_635050609" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/635050609" />
    <a title="Rowena" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2990642-rowena"><img alt="Rowena" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1499471926p2/2990642.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/635050609?book_show_action=true">Jun 06, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Rowena" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Rowena" href="/user/show/2990642-rowena">Rowena</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780140818031" href="/book/show/44595.Wide_Sargasso_Sea">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer635050609" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer147012591474520134"><b> Probably contains some spoilers </b><br/><br/><i> “Our garden was large and beautiful as that garden in the Bible – the tree of life grew there. But it had gone wild. The paths were overgrown and a smell of dead flowers mixed with the fresh living smell. Underneath the tree ferns, tall as forest trees, the light was green. Orchids flourished out of reach or for some reason not to be touched. One was snaky looking, another like an octopus with long thin brown tentacles bare of leaves hanging from a twisted root.</i></span>
  <span id="freeText147012591474520134" style="display:none"><b> Probably contains some spoilers </b><br /><br /><i> “Our garden was large and beautiful as that garden in the Bible – the tree of life grew there. But it had gone wild. The paths were overgrown and a smell of dead flowers mixed with the fresh living smell. Underneath the tree ferns, tall as forest trees, the light was green. Orchids flourished out of reach or for some reason not to be touched. One was snaky looking, another like an octopus with long thin brown tentacles bare of leaves hanging from a twisted root.”</i> - Jean Rhys, Wide Sargasso Sea<br /><br /> I was curious to read this book as it was considered a sort of prequel to Jane Eyre. So I guess this counts as fanfiction? At least it’s very well-written fan fiction!<br /><br />The writing style is of course different from Jane Eyre. The depictions of the Caribbean are beautiful. It’s a relatively short book and it tells the story of Mr. Rochester's first wife, Antoinette Cosway, whom he met in Jamaica. The themes explored in the book are very postcolonialism (discusses the relationships between former slaves and slaveowners after Emancipation), identity (Antoinette is Creole and is therefore not accepted by either the blacks or the whites) and madness.<br /><br />I’ve just finished reading a book about the Suffragette movement that looked into historical accounts of insanity in women. I had no idea that the word "hysteria" was first used to describe a supposed mental ailment that women suffered from all because they had a uterus. *sigh* Apart from being frustrated by that piece of pseudoscience, what's also frustrating is the fact that historically a lot of people were unaware that the environment one lives in can make one "crazy." Women in particular, who were often reliant on men and didn’t have their own freedom were obviously more likely to suffer from nervous breakdowns.<br /><br /> I’m pretty sure most readers will change their opinion of Rochester after they read this. I will definitely see him in a less than favourable light when I do re-read Jane Eyre.</span>
  <a data-text-id="147012591474520134" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1064612749" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5183763681064906093">411.	Wide Sargasso Sea – Jean Rhys (1890 – 1979)<br/>Characters: Antoinette Cosway, Tia, Aunt Cora, Grace Poole, Richard Mason, Annette Cosway, Pierre Cosway, Mr Mason, Christophine, Godfrey, Edward Rochester<br/>Abstract: Born into an oppressive, colonialist society, Creole heiress Antoinette Cosway meets a young Englishman who is drawn to her innocent sensuality and beauty. But soon after their marriage, rumors of madness in her family poison his mind against her. He forces Antoinette to conform to his </span>
  <span id="freeText5183763681064906093" style="display:none">411.	Wide Sargasso Sea – Jean Rhys (1890 – 1979)<br />Characters: Antoinette Cosway, Tia, Aunt Cora, Grace Poole, Richard Mason, Annette Cosway, Pierre Cosway, Mr Mason, Christophine, Godfrey, Edward Rochester<br />Abstract: Born into an oppressive, colonialist society, Creole heiress Antoinette Cosway meets a young Englishman who is drawn to her innocent sensuality and beauty. But soon after their marriage, rumors of madness in her family poison his mind against her. He forces Antoinette to conform to his rigid Victorian ideals.<br /><br />تاریخ نخستین خوانش: روز بیست و ششم ماه سپتامبر سال 1992 میلادی<br />عنوان: گردابی چنین هایل؛ نوشته: جین ریس؛ مترجم: گلی امامی؛ مشخصات نشر: تهران، اسپرک، 1370، در 193 ص، کتابنامه به صورت زیرنویس، موضوع: داستانهای نویسندگان انگلیسی - سده 20 م<br />عنوان: گردابی چنین هایل؛ نوشته: جین ریس؛ مترجم: گلی امامی؛ مشخصات نشر: تهران، کتاب پنجره: 1387، در 210 صفحه<br /><br />رمان «گردابی چنین هایل» پس از سالها دوباره روانه بازار کتاب شد. برگردان همین رمان، نخستین بار توسط نشر «اسپرک» به سال 1370 هجری خورشیدی، منتشر شده بود. مقدمه‌ ی فرانسیس ویندهام، خوانشگر را با زندگی نویسنده، بانو «جین ریس» آشنا می‌کند. نویسنده‌ ی متولد هندغربی، که بیشتر سال‌های کاری خود را در انگلستان گذرانده، در زمانه‌ ی خودش در نیمه‌ ی نخست سده ی بیستم میلادی، شهرتی به سزا داشته اند. سبک نو نوشته‌ های ایشان، خود را، برابر با رمان‌هایی نشان می‌دهند، که در همان سال‌ها، به قلم بانوان نویسنده ی انگلیسی منتشر شده‌ اند، ناقلان آثار و طوطیان شکر شکن شیرین گفتار نیز، چنین آورده اند که: شبا‌هت‌های بسیاری با نوشته‌ های «آندریا لِوِی»، در داستانهای خانوم ریس دیده می‌شود. پایان نقل از طوطیان شکرشکن شیرین گفتار. داستان در نخستین سال‌های سده ی بیستم میلادی ست که آغاز می‌شود. جایی در جامائیکا، زنی تنها با دخترش روزگار می‌گذرانند. آن‌ها روزگاری ثروتمند بوده اند، و از امتیاز زندگی اشرافی خود لذتها برده اند، با لغو برده داری، و درگذشت سرپرست خانواده، در انزوا به سر می‌برند. آن‌ها نه انگلیسی هستند و نه سیاه‌پوست. از سفید‌پوست‌های بومی جزیره هستند. انگلیسی‌ها آن‌ها را کرئول میخوانند، و سیاه‌ها آن‌ها را «سوسک سفید» صدا‌ می‌زنند. دختر روای داستان، روایت را آغاز میکند، تا دنیایی پر از زشتی به تصویر کشد. دنیایی که، زندگی آن‌ها را به آتش کشیده است. در فصل‌های پسین رمان، دختری جوان مقیم صومعه است، سپس ازدواج می‌کند، تا هر دو با هم، رمان را پیش ببرند: باز هم، دنیا پر از سیاهی ست، پر از نفرت است، و پر از جدایی ست. جایی ست که انگار قرار نیست آرامش وجود داشته باشد، تا بتوان لحظه‌ ای آرام بود، و چشم برهم گذاشت. «جین ریس» از رئالیسم شاعرانه‌ ای بهره می‌برند، که هماره خوانشگران علاقه‌ ی ویژه، به نوشته های ایشان نشان می‌دهند. جمله‌ های بلند، و البته پر از صنایع ادبی، که وظیفه‌ ی تصویرسازی را به عهده دارند. اثر همانند نوشته‌ های امروزین، در ذهن راویان اصلی داستان چرخ می‌زند، و مرتب با عوض شدن راوی، ماجرا از چند سو دنبال، و تصاویر به ذهن خوانشگر فرستاده می‌شوند. ا. شربیانی</span>
  <a data-text-id="5183763681064906093" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_1259706130" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1259706130" />
    <a title="Michael" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/32804270-michael"><img alt="Michael" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1564311252p2/32804270.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1259706130?book_show_action=true">Feb 18, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Michael" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Michael" href="/user/show/32804270-michael">Michael</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780393308808" href="/book/show/481558.Wide_Sargasso_Sea">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32804270-michael?shelf=2020">2020</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32804270-michael?shelf=recs">recs</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1259706130" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17445887682057308514">Hazy and full of dread, <i>Wide Sargasso Sea</i> fleshes out the character of Antoinette, the first wife of Rochester from <i>Jane Eyre</i>. Set in Jamaica shortly after the abolition of slavery, the novel follows from birth to death the heroine, a French creole woman of the former planter class who finds herself estranged from white and Black communities alike because of her fraught social identity. In lush, fragmented prose Rhys begins and ends the story from the perspective of Antoinette, who speaks ellipt</span>
  <span id="freeText17445887682057308514" style="display:none">Hazy and full of dread, <i>Wide Sargasso Sea</i> fleshes out the character of Antoinette, the first wife of Rochester from <i>Jane Eyre</i>. Set in Jamaica shortly after the abolition of slavery, the novel follows from birth to death the heroine, a French creole woman of the former planter class who finds herself estranged from white and Black communities alike because of her fraught social identity. In lush, fragmented prose Rhys begins and ends the story from the perspective of Antoinette, who speaks elliptically of her traumatic childhood in the colony and her descent into madness in England. The middle, longest part centers on Rochester over the course of his honeymoon, and carefully charts the psychology of a money-hungry white abuser who’s happy to exploit his wife for all she’s worth, then cage her out of sight. The work’s end, with its self-conscious restaging of pivotal scenes from <i>Jane Eyre</i>, leaves something to be desired, but otherwise it’s an arresting slow burn.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17445887682057308514" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_210858692" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/210858692" />
    <a title="Nandakishore Varma" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6237864-nandakishore-varma"><img alt="Nandakishore Varma" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1583648733p2/6237864.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/210858692?book_show_action=true">Sep 19, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Nandakishore Varma" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Nandakishore Varma" href="/user/show/6237864-nandakishore-varma">Nandakishore Varma</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Mass Market Paperback " href="/book/show/7889496-wide-sargasso-sea">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6237864-nandakishore-varma?shelf=general-fiction">general-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer210858692" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14187217259221421450">Every once in a while, I stop to think about the neglected characters in various novels who exist only as plot devices. What are their stories? If you saw the novel through their eyes, what would it be like?<br/><br/>Therefore, ever since I heard the premise of Jean Rhys's novel, I was eager to read it. Bertha, Mr. Rochester's first wife, must have had a life other than as the "madwoman in the attic". I do not know if Charlotte Bronte ever thought about it, but Ms. Rhys obviously did, and this compellingl</span>
  <span id="freeText14187217259221421450" style="display:none">Every once in a while, I stop to think about the neglected characters in various novels who exist only as plot devices. What are their stories? If you saw the novel through their eyes, what would it be like?<br /><br />Therefore, ever since I heard the premise of Jean Rhys's novel, I was eager to read it. Bertha, Mr. Rochester's first wife, must have had a life other than as the "madwoman in the attic". I do not know if Charlotte Bronte ever thought about it, but Ms. Rhys obviously did, and this compellingly readable novel is the product.<br /><br />The language is beautifully evocative. I could see the West Indies, even though I have never been there. I could see, hear and smell the tropical countryside (very much like my homeland), at once breathtakingly beautiful, compellingly seductive and strangely frightening-like Antoinette. Especially to the eyes of an Englishman whose green meadows and rolling fields hold no secrets.<br /><br />Yes, the countryside is beautiful... but dangerous, since you can get lost in it. It may suddenly cloud over and start to rain, and you may find yourself in the burnt-out ruins of a country house populated only by ghosts of dead slaves and murdered slave-owners.<br /><br />The characterisation is perfect. Rhys draws each character, including the minor ones, with a few deft brush strokes. Rochester, for all his faults, comes across as sympathetic, a victim of his times and society: the "evils" he does are part of his social makeup. And Antoinette is a masterpiece-inseparable from the landscape she inhabits. As we progress through the novel and she slips more and more into madness, the narrative also matches her mental state. In fact, the third part is downright creepy.<br /><br />However, I am still plagued by a niggling doubt... would this novel be effective for someone totally ignorant of <i>Jane Eyre</i>?<br /><br />Oh well...maybe the question is irrelevant.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14187217259221421450" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer3478685976458814848">Forgive me, Dear Reader, for this rather shocking confession, but this Reader has never read <i>Jane Eyre</i>. As a lover of Literature (I guess for the time being I should demote myself to ‘so-called lover of Literature’) I stand before you hanging my head in shame (just ignore the fact that there’s a book laying in my lap). It seems to be a rather troubling trend as before this I read J.M. Coetzee’s <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/525544.Foe' title='Foe by J.M. Coetzee' rel='nofollow noopener'>Foe</a>, without ever having read <i>Robinson Crusoe.</i>. <br/><br/>So, having never read <i>JE</i>, how can it be that I simply </span>
  <span id="freeText3478685976458814848" style="display:none">Forgive me, Dear Reader, for this rather shocking confession, but this Reader has never read <i>Jane Eyre</i>. As a lover of Literature (I guess for the time being I should demote myself to ‘so-called lover of Literature’) I stand before you hanging my head in shame (just ignore the fact that there’s a book laying in my lap). It seems to be a rather troubling trend as before this I read J.M. Coetzee’s <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/525544.Foe" title="Foe by J.M. Coetzee" rel="nofollow noopener">Foe</a>, without ever having read <i>Robinson Crusoe.</i>. <br /><br />So, having never read <i>JE</i>, how can it be that I simply adore this book? In the time I spent rereading this novel (that’s right, I read it twice), should I not have been reading Miss Brontë instead? Is it possible that I enjoyed it more, having never read <i>JE</i>? I’m afraid I do not have the answers you may seek. I feel better already having gotten this off my chest, but rest assured I will appeal to the Literature Gods for further guidance in this matter. I do not wish to lead anyone astray, but if <i>JE</i> is holding preventing from picking this one up all I can say (whisper, really) is: DO IT! (I was going to say JUST DO IT, but someone already trademarked that.)</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer6208566630184255022">An epic romance made meek, singular, aromatic, ethereal, surreal. A fresh little nugget of splendor, of much-needed prose perfection. This is gothic romance at its absolute height. (It's perhaps the best piece of fan-fiction ever.) & I say this as "WSS" is in actuality a side story formulated for the emblematic crazed woman smack in the middle of "Jane Eyre". But it takes a life of its own... merging elements of brutal nature and brutal nurture both, to birth a spectacle like one I've never expe</span>
  <span id="freeText6208566630184255022" style="display:none">An epic romance made meek, singular, aromatic, ethereal, surreal. A fresh little nugget of splendor, of much-needed prose perfection. This is gothic romance at its absolute height. (It's perhaps the best piece of fan-fiction ever.) &amp; I say this as "WSS" is in actuality a side story formulated for the emblematic crazed woman smack in the middle of "Jane Eyre". But it takes a life of its own... merging elements of brutal nature and brutal nurture both, to birth a spectacle like one I've never experienced before. Not short of magical, it's baffling how truly impactful these short novels really are. Rhys gives us so much by giving us the absolute least. Leaving the reader naturally to ask for more. <br /><br />There are specks of Graham Greene (the impeccable) here; as well as Toni Morrison (the visionary)-- SO the best of the best in the best.<br /><br />To be read IMMEDIATELY. "Wide Sargasso Sea": certainly a masterpiece.</span>

  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >
    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_711331732" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/711331732" />
    <a title="Cecily" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1199525-cecily"><img alt="Cecily" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1480285828p2/1199525.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/711331732?book_show_action=true">Sep 04, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Cecily" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Cecily" href="/user/show/1199525-cecily">Cecily</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780140274219" href="/book/show/44597.Wide_Sargasso_Sea">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1199525-cecily?shelf=mental-health-victorian-madness">mental-health-victorian-madness</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1199525-cecily?shelf=miscellaneous-fiction">miscellaneous-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1199525-cecily?shelf=race-people-of-colour">race-people-of-colour</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer711331732" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3325682361512442350">I think the idea of one author piggy-backing, uninvited, on the characters and plot of another, is decidedly dodgy. However, this is widely regarded as a classic, and as I've read Jane Eyre many times (review here: <a target='_blank' rel='noopener nofollow' href='https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/23324130'>http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/...</a>), I thought I should finally try this prequel novella.<br/><br/>With such well-known books, I don't think it's a spoiler to say this imagines the story of the mad first wife in Rochester's attic: from her childhood in Jamaica, through to her marriage to </span>
  <span id="freeText3325682361512442350" style="display:none">I think the idea of one author piggy-backing, uninvited, on the characters and plot of another, is decidedly dodgy. However, this is widely regarded as a classic, and as I've read Jane Eyre many times (review here: <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/23324130">http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/...</a>), I thought I should finally try this prequel novella.<br /><br />With such well-known books, I don't think it's a spoiler to say this imagines the story of the mad first wife in Rochester's attic: from her childhood in Jamaica, through to her marriage to Rochester, and a final epilogue that ties the two novels together, set in her attic at Thorfield. She travels from privilege to poverty and then to something else altogether.<br /><br />NOTE: The book and this review use the N word occasionally (and appropriately, imo).<br /><br />THEMES<br /><br />The novel is set shortly after the emancipation of the plantation slaves, and Antoinette (aka Bertha in Jane Eyre) is the creole daughter of a former slave-owner. <br /><br />Colour (in ever sense), and its contrasts and consequences, is at the heart of the novel, such as when "marooned" is used as a literal description and a metaphor, when Antoinette is looking at her mother. <br /><br />The lush and multitudinous colours of the Carribean ooze from almost every page (see quotes at the end), but it's the colour of people that is more problematic.<br /><br />Antoinette is mixed-race, but mostly accepted as white. Except that "accepted" isn't really true. When her widowed mother marries Mr Mason ("we ate English food now"), she notices how English he is, how un-English her mother is, and is less sure about herself. A black person describes her as a "white cockroach" and the English think of her as a "white nigger", but the blacks say that a "black nigger is better than white nigger". She muses, "I often wonder who I am and where is my country and where do I belong".<br /><br />Colour determined the balance of power in colonies like Jamaica. Freeing the slaves changed that, but didn't entirely reverse it. With the story set at this turning point, it's not only Antoinette who is questioning her identity and her place in life, and to some extent, her personal change of circumstances, and Rochester's role in that, echo those of colonial people in general. <br /><br />The rich colours sometimes have an unreality about them, and that seems prescient when dreams and drugs appear to muddle reality and unreality ("Only the magic and the dream are true — all the rest's a lie."). But they are transient, I think.<br /><br />What of madness? I was expecting this book to be about what madness means, and the use and abuse of the label (especially by men, about women), and perhaps it is. Nevertheless, the overwhelming theme for me was colour.<br /><br />NARRATORS - AND VICTIMS<br /><br />The story is initially told by Antoinette. Later Rochester has a turn, and they swap a couple more times. In narration and dialogue, it wasn't always immediately clear who was talking. Not a huge problem, but a definite irritation.<br /><br />More provocatively, if Antoinette really IS mad, how can she tell her story so coherently, and if she ISN'T really mad...? In fact, Rochester's final passage is more muddled and rambling than any of Antoinette's.<br /><br />Given this, and the way Rochester was tricked into the marriage (stated in Jane Eyre, but given detail here) and some of what happens in this book (<a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[rumours and lies to turn him against his wife, black magic, potions <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>), one has to ask whether he is as much of a victim as Antoinette is. <br /><br />LINKS WITH JANE EYRE<br /><br />The heat of this book is in stark contrast with the cold that pervades much of Jane Eyre: here the passions are out in the open; in that, they are often suppressed. <br /><br />Apart from the characters (though Rochester is never named), there are echoes of what is to come, perhaps designed to reinforce local ideas of of the power of obeah (magic): <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[arson causes the family to flee their home; her mother apparently goes mad (from shock and grief); manic laughter; nightmares; a nearly cancelled wedding <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>. The colour red is strong in both, in the literal sense (furnishings, flowers, fabrics, sky etc), and perhaps as a foreboding of fire of temper and of flames.<br /><br />A more Biblical omen comes from several mentions of a cock crowing: just before the wedding; when collecting <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[a black-magic potion  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>("That is for betrayal, but who is the traitor?"), and persistently when Rochester is planning taking Antoinette back to Spanish Town. <br /><br />DANIEL COSWAY<br /><br />Having opened by saying I dislike the idea of adding to another author's story, I was intrigued by Daniel to the extent that if there was a book about him, I might be tempted to pick it up: what sort of man is he really, and what are his motives?<br /><br />Daniel claims to be one of many illegitimate half-siblings of Antoinette. Her (white) father was well-known for his philandering ways with local women, but the authenticity of Daniel's personal claim is disputed. <br /><br />Baptiste tells Rochester that Daniel is "a very superior man, always reading the Bible" and, a few sentences later, "Daniel is a bad man and he will come here and make trouble". Is this poor editing, or deliberate and significant?<br /><br />In his house, there is a framed text reading "Vengeance is mine" and Daniel is jealous of Sandi (the favoured illegitimate son, who passes as white, and is wealthy). His feelings about Antoinette are less clear, and his motives for telling Rochester about her mother's madness and other gossip are also uncertain: does he want to protect Antoinette in some way, is he hoping Rochester will be so incensed that he will wreak revenge on the Masons (and maybe gratitude on Daniel) and why, at the end of the scene, <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ does he ask Rochester for hush money, when what he's said is apparently common knowledge? <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>. Or maybe I'm reading too much into a minor scene?<br /><br />QUOTES<br /><br />* "The diamond-shaped pieces of silk melted one into the other, red, blue, purple, green, yellow, all one shimmering colour."<br /><br />* An extraordinary oxymoron about her convent school: "my refuge, a place of sunshine and death". <br /><br />* "light and dark, sunshine and shadow, Heaven and Hell"<br /><br />* "Everything is too much... Too much blue, too much purple, too much green. The flowers too red, the mountains too high, the hills too near." <br /><br />* A bathing pool has "secret loveliness. And it kept its secret... I want what it hides."<br /><br />* "It was often raining when I woke during the night, a light capricious shower, dancing playful rain, or hushed, muted, growing louder, more persistent, more powerful, an inexorable sound. But always music, a music I had never heard before."<br /><br />* "I hated her. For she belonged to the magic and the loveliness. She had left me thirsty and all my life would be thirst and longing for what I had lost before I found it."<br /><br /><br />RATING<br /><br />I'm really torn between 3* and 4* - and I see the GR rating averages 3.51* On balance, I think 4* - but only just. </span>
  <a data-text-id="3325682361512442350" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>
