<div id="review_619572645" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/619572645" />
    <a title="Dolors" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/18383778-dolors"><img alt="Dolors" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1502350013p2/18383778.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/619572645?book_show_action=true">May 19, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Dolors" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Dolors" href="/user/show/18383778-dolors">Dolors</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780140274219" href="/book/show/44597.Wide_Sargasso_Sea">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">lovers of playing with fire</span></div>

          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Dolors by: </span>
      the voices
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/18383778-dolors?shelf=read-in-2013">read-in-2013</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer619572645" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17790706293580419299">Fear of the fallen myth syndrome is what has prevented me from reading this book for years. <br/>You have to understand, <i>Jane Eyre</i> was my first "adult" novel. I was still a tomboy who had only read Enid Blyton's "The Secret Seven" when one scorching summer day the torn spine of a seemingly ancient book caught my attention among a few volumes sitting on my Godmother's shelves. I remember that summer as one of the best of my life, and while Jane became my personal heroine and I developed a fervent crus</span>
  <span id="freeText17790706293580419299" style="display:none">Fear of the fallen myth syndrome is what has prevented me from reading this book for years. <br />You have to understand, <i>Jane Eyre</i> was my first "adult" novel. I was still a tomboy who had only read Enid Blyton's "The Secret Seven" when one scorching summer day the torn spine of a seemingly ancient book caught my attention among a few volumes sitting on my Godmother's shelves. I remember that summer as one of the best of my life, and while Jane became my personal heroine and I developed a fervent crush on Mr. Rochester, I discovered an awkward but exhilarating female awareness completely foreign to me. <br />Call me nostalgic, but I didn't want to lose that simple and uncomplicated feelings from my childhood. <br />I should have known better. <br /><br />Doomed Bertha, from fire you come, consumed in fire you will go. <br />Antoinette Cosway is the scapegoat of a decadent, expatriate and exploited society. She neither belongs with the "whites" nor the "niggers". She is a <i>white cockroach</i>, a <i>white nigger</i> who carries the heavy burden of her slave-owner ancestors during the last remains of the British and French Colonialism in the Caribbean Islands. <br />A neglected child, she blossoms in isolated existence, nurturing a strange communion with the exotic, humid landscape of her home in Coulibri. Prejudice and superstition ignite her dormant sinister tendencies when her mother's house is set on fire by the locals, killing his half-witted brother Pierre and leaving Mr. Mason, her mother's second husband, as her sole protector. <br />A young woman who shies from light and longs for the cool shadows of a convent where she recovers from shock. Her beauty thrives attuned to the hostile and oppressing tropical surroundings.<br /><b> One should never leave lonely alone. </b> <br /><b>Mrs. Rochester</b>, with her sad, dark alien eyes. And her red dress, as blinding as furious flames but in complete harmony with the <i> too much red flowers</i>, closes in on her young husband, choking him, disorienting him. <br /><br />Impressionable Edward Fairfax Rochester, the typical Victorian gentleman, overwhelmed with the intensity of these somber, bizarre, damp landscapes with its menacing and reproachful people, can't avoid feeling equally appalled and attracted to his new wife. His cold and possessive masculinity keeps him from acting far from virtuously with his young wife, pressing instead of soothing, demanding instead of empathizing, presumptuous instead of trusting. <br /><br />Clash of cultures, class and race relations, the suffocating subconscious of unknown misdeeds...history makes a victim out of Antoinette. Deranged woman? Or simply a collateral undesired effect of the oppression of colonialism and patriarchal tradition, embodied of course, in Mr. Rochester? <br />Jean Rhys masters the helplessly biased, non reliable, first person narrative form, alternating the voices of both Antoinette and Edward, smothering the reader with her misleading lush tone, hypnotizing him with her exquisite and deeply disturbing style, which builds up imperceptibly towards a suffocating and unavoidable culmination. <br />Antoinette says <i>
  <b>It is always too late for truth.</b>
</i><br />Truth. Such an elusive word. We all seek truth. But which truth? <br />I have found my own truth, better said, I have shaken hands with my long time found but not avowed truth. A truth I already perceived in Mr. Rochester's guilt, in his deceitful ways, in his rough domineering character. He never loved Antoinette, Bertha as he called her in a futile attempt to change her, for she was much of a wild spirit, caught alight, impossible to cage. She didn't have a place in this world, so Mr. Rochester forced her to create her own. <br />And curiously, this new found truth does nothing but enhance my feelings for my childhood hero, this wounded rogue, shinning with all his flaws, who comes more alive today than ever. <br />I should have known better. Rhys' novel stands on its own and it's her subtle and haunting voice that allows us to listen to those who never dared to speak before. I have listened and understood. Have you?<br /><br /><b>I hated the mountains and the hills, the rivers and the rain. I hated the sunsets of whatever colour, I hated its beauty and its magic and the secret I would never know. I hated its indifference and the cruelty which was part of its loveliness. Above all I hated her. For she belonged to the magic and the loveliness. She had left me thirsty and all my life would be thirst and longing for what I had lost before I found it.</b>.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="17790706293580419299" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

  <span id="freeText14489953124220611766" style="display:none"> <br /><b>Oct.11,1964</b> <br /> <br /><i>Sitting in bed. Scribbling. Using a pencil instead of pen for the ink spills over while I shake. Influence of cheap wine. <br /><br />Sometimes I get out of control, freaky. My neighbors think I am mad. Ha! What do they know of madness? Who knows of madness? People only see what is there before their eyes. Who bothers to think how the despair creeps inside, shutting out the doors to the World permanently? <br /><br />I look at the copy of Jane Eyre kept on the table by my side. I fill with rage. <br /><br />No one thought of you. No emotion or thoughts expressed, not even fear. No one thought you had a soul too; a heart that once throbbed to the love of a man. Or that you loved to sing and dance, perhaps. Or that you had a mother, consumed by a raging fire of the memory of your dead brother. That you tried to keep sane in a mad-mad world filled with people who were no better.<br /><br />Why did no one think of you, Bertha? No, not Bertha. I won’t call you that. It is how they have known you, till now. Only a madwoman in the attic. I will call you Antoinette, a name your mother may have given you, with love, when you were born. <br /><br />I’ll tell your side of the story. The other side.</i><br /><br />--------------------------------------------------------<br /><br />Disclaimer: It is a fictional attempt to imagine and understand how Rhys <br />might have felt about writing for the madwoman, the writing being done while living in isolation at a small place for almost 20 years.<br /><br /><br /> <br /></span>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2211536760?book_show_action=true">Dec 11, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Matthew" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Matthew" href="/user/show/16254355-matthew">Matthew</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780393308808" href="/book/show/481558.Wide_Sargasso_Sea">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/16254355-matthew?shelf=classic">classic</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/16254355-matthew?shelf=completist-book-club">completist-book-club</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/16254355-matthew?shelf=library">library</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2211536760" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6385171293420085105">I don't think I really understood this book at first, but after I finished it, I went looking around online for more info about it and it clicked. This book is a prequel to Jane Eyre to be read after you read Jane Eyre. Reading it before you read Jane Eyre will probably spoil some of it for you. Also, as a stand alone book without referrence to Jane Eyre, I don't think it is a particularly interesting book.<br/><br/>The story for me was a bit flat. I didn't fully understand the motivation of the character</span>
  <span id="freeText6385171293420085105" style="display:none">I don't think I really understood this book at first, but after I finished it, I went looking around online for more info about it and it clicked. This book is a prequel to Jane Eyre to be read after you read Jane Eyre. Reading it before you read Jane Eyre will probably spoil some of it for you. Also, as a stand alone book without referrence to Jane Eyre, I don't think it is a particularly interesting book.<br /><br />The story for me was a bit flat. I didn't fully understand the motivation of the characters. This is a case of having to know a bit of the background of the world at the time the book was released to really get it. There are a lot of themes around the treatment of women, race, slavery, imperialism, etc. that the casual reader may not catch. I know I didn't at first (thanks, Wikipedia! 😉)<br /><br />So, here's a basic bullet point rundown:<br /><br />- A so-so story<br />- Doesn't really stand well on it's own (must be read after Jane Eyre)<br />- With Jane Eyre in mind, you should really read a synopsis of it before, during, and/or after you read this (unless you are well versed in Jane Eyre)<br />- I give it 3 stars as it is cool to get backstory from another book, but I couldn't go higher because I was not moved or blown away. The story just was there . . . kinda blah.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6385171293420085105" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_3471265561" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3471265561" />
    <a title="leynes" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/45198798-leynes"><img alt="leynes" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1593512704p2/45198798.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3471265561?book_show_action=true">Aug 09, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="leynes" class="user" itemprop="url" name="leynes" href="/user/show/45198798-leynes">leynes</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3471265561" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6104962624608392516">My crops are flourishing. My skin is clear. My grades are up. I couldn't be happier. <i>Wide Sargasso Sea</i> is the gift that keeps on giving, and I will forever be grateful to Jean Rhys for finally doing what needed to be done. Charlotte Brontë could never. <br/><br/>In case you didn't know, <i>Wide Sargasso Sea</i> (1966) is a feminist and anti-colonial response to Charlotte Brontë's novel <i>Jane Eyre</i> (1847), describing the background to Mr. Rochester's marriage from the point-of-view of his "mad wife" Antoinette Cosw</span>
  <span id="freeText6104962624608392516" style="display:none">My crops are flourishing. My skin is clear. My grades are up. I couldn't be happier. <i>Wide Sargasso Sea</i> is the gift that keeps on giving, and I will forever be grateful to Jean Rhys for finally doing what needed to be done. Charlotte Brontë could never. <br /><br />In case you didn't know, <i>Wide Sargasso Sea</i> (1966) is a feminist and anti-colonial response to Charlotte Brontë's novel <i>Jane Eyre</i> (1847), describing the background to Mr. Rochester's marriage from the point-of-view of his "mad wife" Antoinette Cosway (aka Bertha Mason). <br /><br />Despite it being my mom's favorite book, I only got interested in the novel after I heard Marlon James gushing about it on his podcast. He said: <b>"In a lot of ways it was a novel that finally slammed the door shut on the 19th century. You can't read <i>Wide Sargasso Sea</i> and then read Victorian literature with their rose curtains anymore. You realize that these are some nasty hypocritical people who destroy everybody in their way who are not them."</b> I mean, that's sure as hell a way to get me interested in a book, Marlon. Sign me the fuck up! <br /><br />On top of that, I've been getting really interested into the postcolonial literary practice of "writing back" – so postcolonial writers responding to texts in the canon by taking silenced characters from the margins and placing them in the center by giving them a voice and more background in their own narratives, and <i>Wide Sargasso Sea</i> is like the textbook example when it comes to writing back. <br /><br />In <i>Jane Eyre</i>, Bertha Mason doesn't speak. Not a single word. She doesn't have a voice. All she does is growl and hiss. There are only a few scenes where we can catch a glimpse of her and there are also not that many scenes where she is being talked about. However, as a little refresher, let's look at at how Bertha is portrayed in <i>Jane Eyre</i>. When Rochester's illegal wedding to Jane gets interrupted by an intruder who declares: "The marriage cannot go on: I declare the existence of an impediment.", we first learn of the existence of Rochester's wife. After a moment of inarticulate fury, Rochester admits that his wife is alive and that in marrying Jane he would have been knowingly taking a second wife. He then proceeds to describe his wife in these charming terms:<blockquote>
  <b>Bertha Mason is mad; and she came of a mad family; idiots and maniacs through three generations! Her mother, the Creole, was both a madwoman and a drunkard!—as I found out after I had wed the daughter; for they were silent on family secrets before. Bertha, like a dutiful child, copied her parent in both points. I had a charming partner—pure, wise, modest; you can fancy I was a happy man. I went through rich scenes! Oh! my experience has been heavenly, if you only knew it! But I owe you no further explanation. Briggs, Wood, Mason, I invite you all to come up to the house and visit Mrs. Poole's patient, and my wife! You shall see what sort of a being I was cheated into espousing, and judge whether or not I had a right to break the compact, and seek sympathy with something at least human.</b>
</blockquote>So, he is not only describing her (and her family) in incredibly insulting terms (by being downright mean, dismissive and condescending), he also dehumanises her in the most horrid fashion (as can be seen in the last sentence of that quote^^). As if this wasn't bad enough we then get to see Bertha in action as Rochester orders the crowd to come to Thornfield to see her, to judge for themselves:<blockquote>
  <b>In the deep shade, at the farther end of the room, a figure ran backwards and forwards. What it was, whether beast or human being, one could not, at first sight, tell: it grovelled, seemingly, on all fours; it snatched and growled like some strange wild animal: but it was covered with clothing, and a quantity of dark, grizzled hair, wild as a mane, hid its head and face.</b>
</blockquote>Here again, Bertha is seen through a colonial gaze and gets thus dehumanised. She is seen as a "beast", a "wild animal" who is devoid of speech. She is perceived as being dangerous, uncontrollable and maniac. A few moments later, Jane describes: "Mr Rochester flung me behind him: the lunatic sprang and grappled his throat viciously, and laid her teeth to his cheek." <br /><br />And that's pretty much all we get of Bertha in <i>Jane Eyre</i>, except for the part where she burns Rochester's fucking house down (of course) and flings herself to her own death (how fucking convenient, right?). <br /><br />So, it comes as no surprise that Jean Rhys, a white Creole woman herself, took offense to that portrayal. In one letter, she wrote: "The Creole in Charlotte Brontë's novel is a lay figure - repulsive which does not matter, and not once alive which does. She's necessary to the plot, but always she shrieks, howls, laughs horribly, attacks all and sundry - off stage. For me (and for you I hope), she must be on stage. She must be at least plausible with a past, the reason why Mr. Rochester treats her so abominably and feels justified, the reason why he thinks she is mad and why of course she goes mad, even the reason why she tries to set everything on fire, and eventually succeeds. Personally, I think that one is simple. She is cold - and fire is the only warmth she knows in England."<blockquote>
  <b>“Is there another side?” <br />“There is always the other side, always.”</b>
</blockquote>Jean Rhys tells Antoinette's story from the time of her youth in Jamaica, to her unhappy marriage to a certain unnamed English gentleman, who renames her Bertha, declares her mad, takes her to England, and isolates her away from the rest of the world in his mansion. [Sounds familiar? No. Then reread <i>Jane Eyre</i> with your eyes fucking open this time.] Rhys uses multiple voices (Antoinette's, her husband's, and Grace Poole's) to tell the story, and intertwines her novel's plot with that of <i>Jane Eyre</i>. In addition, Rhys makes a postcolonial argument when she ties Antoinette's husband's eventual rejection of Antoinette to her Creole heritage (a rejection shown to be critical to Antoinette's descent into madness). <br /><br />But Jean Rhys does a lot more than just revising <i>Jane Eyre</i> – she doesn't simply flesh out the characters of the original novel to explain their motivations, she actually intervenes in the very project of the novel and its imposing structure, exposing its historical limitations and its legacy as a product of empire and violence.What <i>Wide Sargasso Sea</i> explores is negotiation of the space between audiences and performers, sanity and madness, expectation and fulfilment, acting and being.<br /><br />The Sargasso Sea lies between Europe (Rochester's home) and the West Indies (Antoinette's home) and is difficult to navigate, like the human situations in the novel. Many vessels have become becalmed or lost in this area of the ocean. Similarly, Antoinette feels lost as she is figuratively caught between England and Jamaica. She is neither colonial nor Jamaican, but a white Creole. The social and racial currents swirl around her as she searches for stability and identity.<blockquote>
  <b>I wonder why I have been brought here. For that reason? There must be a reason. What is it that I must do? When I first came I thought it would be for a day, two days, a week perhaps. I thought that when I saw hime and spoke to him I would be wise as serpents, harmless as doves. ‘I give you all I have freely,’ I would say, ‘and I will not trouble you again if you will let me go.’ But he never came.</b>
</blockquote>When she physically crosses the Sargasso Sea and goes to England she believes the ship had lost its way and that she is not really in England. She completely loses her identity, which points out how wide the Sargasso Sea has been for Antoinette. What haunts the reader of this text is the knowledge of what will happen to Antoinette, and the sense that secrets are hidden because people do not want to see what they see, or know what they know. <br /><br />In England, a total rupture occurs in Antoinette's mind; she has been constructed as the mad Creole by Rochester but she does not recognise that self when she sees it, having returned in imagination to the Caribbean world that is shut up in her, as the red dress hangs in the black press smelling of spices. In her dream she sees the woman with the streaming hair in the mirror as a framed ghost, and drops her candle in fear, setting fire to Rochester’s house.<br /><br />It is apparent that Jean Rhys hears voices that Brontë’s novel pushes to the margins or out of hearing. Caliban’s isle was full of noises, but this isle is full of voices, speaking different languages and different versions of English and French. Edward Said, in <i>Culture and Imperialism</i>, suggests that classic realist fiction develops in Europe in the 19th century because the “power to narrate, or to block other narratives from forming and emerging” is a way of asserting cultural superiority. Rhys was annoyed when she read <i>Jane Eyre</i> because she thought: “That’s only one side – the English side” and she chose to write a prequel, rather than sequel, to <i>Jane Eyre</i> to interpret the colonial underpinning of Rochester’s thought and actions, seeing from the perspective of white Creoles who have been displaced by a new wave of colonizers. The implication is that the Creoles are classified as inferior in the new society. Antoinette’s displacement makes her more conscious of what she is not than of what she is; she even envies the ex-slaves for their sense of self-definition. <br /><br />In Rhys’s version of Rochester’s first marriage he is perplexed by the conflicting narratives that lie below the surface of official colonial policy and practice. In this unfamiliar landscape, he tries his best to assert his power over basically every person he meets, but especially over his wife-to-be.<blockquote>
  <b>Bertha is not my name. You are trying to make me into someone else, calling me by another name. I know, that’s obeah too.”</b>
</blockquote>The uncanny control he exercises over her derives from his power as a patriarchal Victorian who has stopped listening to all the islands’ voices. In his fear of Christophine’s obeah magic and the passion between him and Antoinette he has reduced his wife, who loves him, to a spiritless shell: “Like a doll. Even when she threatened me with the bottle she had a marionette equality.”<br /><br />The voice of his suppressed wishes [= the wish to possess his wife], which is heard when he speaks to the ‘witch’ Christophine, seems to be casting and incantatory spell: “Marionette, Antoinette, Marionetta, Antoinetta.” Christophine recognizes what he is doing and accuses him of spirit theft: “All you want is to break her up. She tell me in the middle of all this you start calling her names. Marionette. That word mean doll, eh?” He loathes her otherness, and reveals himself as a practitioner of <b>colonial obeah</b>. With the confidence of his belief in his own cultural and racial superiority he has stolen her spirit and driven her mad, but his vindictive account of it is as unhinged as her behaviour. <br /><br />His lust for possession betrays Antoinette, who gives herself freely to him until he rejects the gift; when Christophine suggests that he and Antoinette should separate so that Antoinette can live happily with some else “a pang of rage and jealousy” shoots through Rochester and in revenge he asserts his ownership of his wife and all her belongings. <br /><br />In <i>Wide Sargasso Sea</i>, Rhys also draws attention to colonialism and the slave trade by which Antoinette's ancestors had made their fortune. The novel does not shy away from uncomfortable truths about British history that had been neglected in Brontë's narrative. The lurking, suggested, but mostly unspeakable secrets of <i>Wide Sargasso Sea</i> are glimpsed but never clearly seen. They are rooted in West Indian history; the islands’ inhabitants seem to Rochester to collude in concealing their past – the willed amnesia of the descendants of both plantation owners, like Antoinette, and of slaves. It is as if both exploiters and exploited want to suppress any record of the humiliation and cruelty of slavery, and yet the landscape is haunted by it.<br /><br />There is a similar resolute amnesia about the transition from slavery to emancipation, when the white Creoles lost their status in comparison with new arrivals from Britain like Mason. Part of this guilt relates to anxiety about miscegenation. Mason buys Rochester for Antoinette because he wants the innuendoes about her and her mother to be silenced, though doubts occur to Rochester even before he hears Daniel’s story. He looks at his wife as they arrive on the honeymoon island and wonders why he has refused to admit what he sees: “Long, sad, dark alien eyes. Creole of pure English descent she may be, but they are not English or European either.”<br /><br />Rochester is so preoccupied by anxieties about incest and racial impurity that he wants to rationalise his own feelings. Though he shows tenderness for her he anxiously assures himself, in the present tense, that desire is not love and that he has no feeling for her because she is different: “I did not love her. I was thirsty for her, but that is not love. I felt very little tenderness for her, she was a stranger to me, a stranger who did not think or feel as I did.”<br /><br />Rhys’s decision to set the main action after the Emancipation Act of 1833 is deliberate, as it connects Antoinette’s quest for selfhood not to Jane’s but to the fate of the ex-slaves of the island. “Qui est la?” recurs as a question in Antoinette’s dreams, echoing the French patois spoken by her surrogate mother figure Christophine, an outsider herself as an ex-slave born in Martinique and living in Jamaica. “Who is there?” is followed by “You frightened?,” and these two challenges drive Antoinette to ultimately choose her Caribbean identity over her European one.<blockquote>
  <b>Now at last I know why I was brought here and what I have to do. There must have been a draught for the flame flickered and I thought it was out. But I shielded it with my hand it burned up again to light me along the dark passage.</b>
</blockquote>When she sets her fatal blaze at Thornfield Hall, it’s because she imagines a red dress spilling across the floor like a flame, recalling an episode in which Christophine tells her to wear a red dress instead of a white one bought for her by Rochester. When asked “You frightened?” for the last time in a hallucination, it is by Tia, a Jamaican girl who was her friend before a mob descended on Antoinette’s childhood plantation home, burning it to the ground. In response to Tia’s call, Antoinette jumps toward her out on the mansion’s ramparts, and the novel ends. The fire that consumes her is directly linked to the one set by that group of newly freed ex-slaves. It is an act of resistance, not madness.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6104962624608392516" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <a title="Mutasim Billah " class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/20438238-mutasim-billah"><img alt="Mutasim Billah " src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1536682441p2/20438238.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2473196747?book_show_action=true">Jun 06, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mutasim Billah " class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mutasim Billah " href="/user/show/20438238-mutasim-billah">Mutasim Billah </a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780141182858" href="/book/show/829372.Wide_Sargasso_Sea">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/20438238-mutasim-billah?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/20438238-mutasim-billah?shelf=west-indies">west-indies</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2473196747" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer507989170687440254"><i>“If I was bound for hell, let it be hell. No more false heavens. No more damned magic. You hate me and I hate you. We’ll see who hates best. But first, first I will destroy your hatred. Now. My hate is colder, stronger, and you’ll have no hate to warm yourself. You will have nothing.”</i><br/><br/>And that pretty much sums up the story. <i>Wide Sargasso Sea</i> is a tale of passion, and madness. Its a story from a time when slavery was abolished and slave traders were shunned from the community in Jamaica. A little </span>
  <span id="freeText507989170687440254" style="display:none"><i>“If I was bound for hell, let it be hell. No more false heavens. No more damned magic. You hate me and I hate you. We’ll see who hates best. But first, first I will destroy your hatred. Now. My hate is colder, stronger, and you’ll have no hate to warm yourself. You will have nothing.”</i><br><br>And that pretty much sums up the story. <i>Wide Sargasso Sea</i> is a tale of passion, and madness. Its a story from a time when slavery was abolished and slave traders were shunned from the community in Jamaica. A little girl grows up and finds herself alienated from society, growing up away from love, as certain events tear her family apart. A world of superstition and magic, a world of vivid colors is where our story is told. A world of hatred, and madness.<br><blockquote>
  <br>
  
    <br>
    <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1592160375i/29646798.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
    <br>
    
      <i>“And what does anyone know about traitors, or why Judas did what he did?”</i>
    
    <br>
  
  <br>
</blockquote><br>The story was written during a period of obscurity in Rhys' career between 1939's <i>Good Morning Midnight</i> and 1966 when this book was released. With an impending World War and economic crisis from the first still at large, the themes of <i>Good Morning Midnight</i> were deemed too depressing by critics and readers and drove her into isolation from the public eye. At such a moment, when she had almost given up writing, an advertisement requesting her whereabouts for permission to adapt her then-latest work into a theatrical presentation appeared in the newspaper. Her friendship with Selma Vaz Dias, the origin of the advertisement, eventually inspired her to begin writing again.<br><br><blockquote>
  <i>“I watched her die many times. In my way, not in hers. In sunlight, in shadow, by moonlight, by candlelight. In the long afternoons when the house was empty. Only the sun was there to keep us company. We shut him out. And why not? Very soon she was as eager for what's called loving as I was - more lost and drowned afterwards.”</i>
</blockquote><br><br><i>Wide Sargasso Sea</i> is written as a feminist, post-colonial prequel to <i>Jane Eyre</i>, one of the two central characters being Antoinette Cosway, shown as a devilish madwoman in the attic in the latter. The central themes are identity politics of the post-Emancipation era, and the story of the women who lived through them, the cultural taboo regarding mental health, and the superstitions of the time, that a slave-trader's daughter's identity is like a double-edged sword. And that love is merely one side of the coin, the other being hatred. I really enjoyed the experience. I hope you do too.<br><br><blockquote>
  <br>
  
    <br>
    <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1592160584i/29646808.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
    <br>
    
      <br>
      <i>“There are always two deaths, the real one and the one people know about.” <br>Image: Karina Lombard as Antoinette Cosway in the 1993 film adaptation of the story.</i>
      <br>
    
    <br>
  
  <br>
</blockquote></span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_924922928" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/924922928" />
    <a title="Caroline " class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3236194-caroline"><img alt="Caroline " src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1576541068p2/3236194.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/924922928?book_show_action=true">Apr 30, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Caroline " class="user" itemprop="url" name="Caroline " href="/user/show/3236194-caroline">Caroline </a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780613175975" href="/book/show/889026.Wide_Sargasso_Sea">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3236194-caroline?shelf=ho-hum">ho-hum</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3236194-caroline?shelf=so-much-potential">so-much-potential</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3236194-caroline?shelf=literary-fiction">literary-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3236194-caroline?shelf=overrated">overrated</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3236194-caroline?shelf=she-wrote-it">she-wrote-it</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3236194-caroline?shelf=waste-of-trees">waste-of-trees</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer924922928" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9347778845879947792">***SPOILERS HIDDEN***<br/>***NO <i>JANE EYRE</i> SPOILERS***<br/><br/>Published 119 years after <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/10210.Jane_Eyre' title='Jane Eyre by Charlotte Brontë' rel='nofollow noopener'>Jane Eyre</a>, the famous classic that inspired it, <i>Wide Sargasso Sea</i> is Jean Rhys’s attempt to give Bertha Mason (here going primarily by the name “Antoinette Cosway”) a detailed back story. A fan of <i>Jane Eyre</i> since I first read it in ninth grade and a fan of the movie version of <i>Wide Sargasso Sea</i>, I greatly looked forward to reading this book. I want to stress how crucial it is to read <i>Jane Eyre</i> before starting this. There's</span>
  <span id="freeText9347778845879947792" style="display:none">***SPOILERS HIDDEN***<br />***NO <i>JANE EYRE</i> SPOILERS***<br /><br />Published 119 years after <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/10210.Jane_Eyre" title="Jane Eyre by Charlotte Brontë" rel="nofollow noopener">Jane Eyre</a>, the famous classic that inspired it, <i>Wide Sargasso Sea</i> is Jean Rhys’s attempt to give Bertha Mason (here going primarily by the name “Antoinette Cosway”) a detailed back story. A fan of <i>Jane Eyre</i> since I first read it in ninth grade and a fan of the movie version of <i>Wide Sargasso Sea</i>, I greatly looked forward to reading this book. I want to stress how crucial it is to read <i>Jane Eyre</i> before starting this. There's simply no understanding what happens in <i>Wide Sargasso Sea</i> otherwise. Though perhaps that doesn't matter, because I don't recommend this anyway. Rhys's basic vision was strong but her execution terrible. <br /><br />To provide some brief background, Rhys imagined Antoinette as a Creole girl in a gorgeously lush and verdant Jamaica, with the story opening at the height of political and racial tensions there. Unfortunately, this atmospheric and complex setting can’t make up for Rhys’s surprisingly weak and hazy portrayal of Antoinette. <br /><br />This prequel is divided into three parts. A young Antoinette narrates part one; part two is narrated mostly by Mr. Rochester (whom Rhys for no valid reason chose to never name); and part three is once again narrated by Antoinette, this time as an adult. <br /><br />Part one starts off promisingly enough, but when Rochester materializes in part two, Antoinette is suddenly an adult woman, newly married to him, and the union is a loveless one. This jump in time is too abrupt to work. Upsetting the smooth flow of part two, Antoinette’s voice randomly interrupts at one point, thus giving the impression that Rhys had trouble organizing this story.<br /><br />The story is most intriguing and moves more quickly when it opens, when young Antoinette is trying to make sense of her relationship with her mother. <i>Here’s</i> a part rich with the potential to add much-needed psychological depth, but Rhys neglected it so sorely that it’s hard to understand why she included anything about their relationship at all. <br /><br />Another trouble spot concerns Rochester. It’s unclear why Rhys allotted him such a generous chunk of the narrative. Rhys didn't flesh out Rochester any more than she fleshed out Antoinette, so significant narrative devoted to him matters little, nor should it change how fans of <i>Jane Eyre</i> feel about Rochester. His portrayal here is simply too colorless to inspire any strong opinions.<br /><br />Rhys’s prose is horrible, both stylistically and technically. It’s choppy and oftentimes convoluted; some punctuation is missing throughout or used incorrectly; and large sections, especially toward the end, veer into nonsensical, difficult to follow, “stream of consciousness”-like territory. I honestly had no idea whatsoever what some of her sentences meant, despite repeated reading. <br /><br />Rhys lacked a fine-tuned ear for dialogue, and she didn't use dialogue to make her characters distinct. It’s striking, for instance, that Rochester’s impressions of their natural surroundings sound remarkably like Antoinette’s:<blockquote><b>Rochester</b>: “One morning soon after we arrived, the row of tall trees outside my window were covered with small pale flowers too fragile to resist the wind. They fell in a day, and looked like snow on the rough grass--snow with a faint sweet scent. Then they were blown away. [...] It was a beautiful place--wild, untouched, above all untouched, with an alien, disturbing, secret loveliness. And it kept its secret.”<p><b>Antoinette</b>: “I can remember every second of that morning, if I shut my eyes I can see the deep blue colour of the sky and the mango leaves, the pink and red hibiscus, the yellow handkerchief she wore round her head, tied in the Martinique fashion with the sharp points in front, but now I see everything still, fixed for ever like the colours in a stained-glass window.”</p></blockquote> By far, though, <i>Wide Sargasso Sea</i>'s biggest misstep is in, well, its very reason for being: its depiction of Antoinette. Her sections should be the most significant, most powerful of the entire book, yet she remains almost exactly as she did in <i>Jane Eyre</i>: a shadow of a woman, indistinct, never able to be truly understood or even pictured. The missed opportunity here for a three-dimensional Antoinette is so very, very disappointing; Rhys only skimmed the surface of this mystery woman’s history. <br /><br />Finally, probably most inexcusable is that Rhys implied that Antoinette’s plunge into insanity <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[could have been avoided had Rochester shown her more love and adoration <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>. This would be laughable if it weren’t so offensive--in more than one way. <br /><br /><i>Wide Sargasso Sea</i> is a disappointment all around and a classic I would have been no worse off for skipping. Anyone eager to know more about the mystery woman from <i>Jane Eyre</i> won’t find the answer in these pages. Bertha Mason, it seems, is a character fated to remain a mystery. Maybe one day a really talented writer will take on the challenge of giving her the backstory Rhys failed at miserably. I'll eagerly read it.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9347778845879947792" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1340552071?book_show_action=true">Jul 20, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sara" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sara" href="/user/show/5512657-sara">Sara</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback " href="/book/show/889028.Wide_Sargasso_Sea">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5512657-sara?shelf=modern-classics">modern-classics</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1340552071" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer826972195528183651">Jean Rhys takes us to the West Indies, an environment that is heavy, languid, stifling, and claustrophobic. It is not surprising that people go insane here, what is surprising is that anyone is able to keep their sanity. In this world of mysticism, racial mixtures and moving boundaries, is born the tragedy that becomes the catalyst to one of the greatest love stories of all time. But that is after--this story belongs, not to the governess, but to the wife.<br/><br/>Antoinette Cosway is a girl who is press</span>
  <span id="freeText826972195528183651" style="display:none">Jean Rhys takes us to the West Indies, an environment that is heavy, languid, stifling, and claustrophobic. It is not surprising that people go insane here, what is surprising is that anyone is able to keep their sanity. In this world of mysticism, racial mixtures and moving boundaries, is born the tragedy that becomes the catalyst to one of the greatest love stories of all time. But that is after--this story belongs, not to the governess, but to the wife.<br /><br />Antoinette Cosway is a girl who is pressed beyond her limits. Subject to her environment and her mother’s precarious hold on her own wits, Antoinette is destined to be a mix of all that is fragile and all that is fierce. She battles, and narrowly wins, until she is sold into marriage to her unlucky bridegroom. His lack of understanding of who she is, and her unconventional worship of him, take her beyond the limits of her abilities to cope, and plunges her into a darkness that is frightful.<br /><br />There is a mix of voodoo, mystery and madness in this novel that is riveting. You pray for help and hope for this poor girl, and yet you know it is not coming, for you know her end before you even begin her story. You have glimpsed her before, almost without pity, but in Rhys’ hands you find the pity and it is all encompassing. <br /><br />Short and sweet and fantastic. One hundred and ninety pages of power. A must-read.</span>
  <a data-text-id="826972195528183651" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_753162229" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

    <a title="Duane" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/24389390-duane"><img alt="Duane" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1403316391p2/24389390.jpg" /></a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780233958668" href="/book/show/889025.Wide_Sargasso_Sea">review of another edition</a>

<span id="freeTextContainer9758202494222512594">Anytime a writer takes on the idea of writing or rewriting another writers story or characters, they are treading on delicate, even sacred ground. Especially in this instance, you are talking about an iconic work, a masterpiece, the gold standard of classic English literature, Jane Eyre, by Charlotte Bronte. But somehow Jean Rhys pulls it off without too much damage to the original work, and let's face it, Bertha needed to have her story told. Bertha's real name is Antoinette Cosway, and this is</span>
  <span id="freeText9758202494222512594" style="display:none">Anytime a writer takes on the idea of writing or rewriting another writers story or characters, they are treading on delicate, even sacred ground. Especially in this instance, you are talking about an iconic work, a masterpiece, the gold standard of classic English literature, Jane Eyre, by Charlotte Bronte. But somehow Jean Rhys pulls it off without too much damage to the original work, and let's face it, Bertha needed to have her story told. Bertha's real name is Antoinette Cosway, and this is her story. A passionate Creole woman who finds herself married to an Englishman, decamped from her Caribbean home to the English countryside, and eventually locked away in the attic of an old English manor and proclaimed insane by her husband.<br /><br />Jean Rhys is an interesting person in her own right. I would think her biography would make for good reading. Unsuccessful most of her life, Wide Sargasso Sea came late in her career, "too late to make a difference" she would say. "If I could live my life over again, and choose......I would rather be happy than write".</span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_542881060" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/542881060" />
    <a title="Cheryl" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/11255849-cheryl"><img alt="Cheryl" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1565547225p2/11255849.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/542881060?book_show_action=true">Nov 09, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Cheryl" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Cheryl" href="/user/show/11255849-cheryl">Cheryl</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780241951552" href="/book/show/10427352-wide-sargasso-sea">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/11255849-cheryl?shelf=women-and-books">women-and-books</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/11255849-cheryl?shelf=historical">historical</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/11255849-cheryl?shelf=the-psyche">the-psyche</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer542881060" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1367326111083691045"><b>"I watched her die many times. In my way, not in hers. In sunlight, in shadow, by moonlight, by candlelight. In the long afternoons when the house was empty. Only the sun was there to keep us company. We shut him out. And why not? Very soon she was as eager for what's called loving as I was - more lost and drowned afterwards."</b><br/><br/>Forget the Jane Eyre parallel, you don't need it. This book encapsulates the melancholy of evolving times and evolving minds and it measures human decency. Just when one th</span>
  <span id="freeText1367326111083691045" style="display:none"><b>"I watched her die many times. In my way, not in hers. In sunlight, in shadow, by moonlight, by candlelight. In the long afternoons when the house was empty. Only the sun was there to keep us company. We shut him out. And why not? Very soon she was as eager for what's called loving as I was - more lost and drowned afterwards."</b><br /><br />Forget the Jane Eyre parallel, you don't need it. This book encapsulates the melancholy of evolving times and evolving minds and it measures human decency. Just when one thinks there is some progression towards human decency, money stills morality. And I'm not just alluding to American politics, I'm also referring to Antoinette, the main character who sees her mother taunted and abused because of her mental disorder, only to watch while the same thing happens to her years later. The common denominators? An enviable house and land, skin color, inherited money from a stepfather (even if she was dirt poor before), black nationalistic pride that determines she is a white minority who must be excavated, despondency of blue-collar employees, and so on. <i>Go,</i> her black housekeeper of many years warns her, <i>take your money and leave your husband and he'll be a poor man.</i> But she doesn't listen to her, something about misplaced trust. What's with the complacency and lack of togetherness that drowns women in deeper mire? <i>Do something.</i> It's coincidental that I read this after reading <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/31548.Of_Human_Bondage" title="Of Human Bondage by W. Somerset Maugham" rel="nofollow noopener">Of Human Bondage</a>, but after watching recent news with tears in my eyes as I had to once again doubt basic human decency, I thought how it must have been fate that linked me with these two works that illustrate life and the oddities of humans and their complexities. <br /><blockquote>I hated the mountains and the hills, the rivers and the rain. I hated the sunsets of whatever color, I hated its beauty and its magic and the secret I would never know. I hated its indifference and the cruelty which was part of its loveliness. Above all I hated her. For she belonged to the magic and the loveliness. She had left me thirsty and all my life would be thirst and longing for what I had lost before I found it. </blockquote><br />It's hard to sympathize with the Cosways who once owned the slaves who birthed those harassing them, but it is not too difficult to empathize with Antoinette who is a child and innocent partaker to the 1830s Jamaican society wanting to erase the brutality of human ownership and colonization. I'm happy that I've read this work of Jean Rhys, because <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/144073.Good_Morning__Midnight" title="Good Morning, Midnight by Jean Rhys" rel="nofollow noopener">Good Morning, Midnight</a> was not one of my favorites. The mood of this book is sharpened through terse, yet visceral prose that proceed in a fragmentary haphazard and hallucinatory manner, in order to elucidate the renderings of psychological upheaval. It is an underscoring of generations who refuse to break the pattern of suffering. Antoinette wants to leave, just like her mother before her, and yet she doesn't until she is forced to. She has money, unlike her mother before her, and yet she, a white woman, allows herself to become a slave to the white men around her: her half-brother who turns over her wealth to his best friend, and a husband who uses her and cares nothing for her well-being. In the end, Antoinette is no different than those of Creole descent around her who for years, fought to gain back their basic human rights, including the right to individualism. Yes, there is something brutal, yet self-actualizing about watching history repeat itself. <br /><i>
  <blockquote>As for my confused impressions they will never be written. There are blanks in my mind that cannot be filled up.</blockquote>
</i></span>
  <a data-text-id="1367326111083691045" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_399318048" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/399318048" />
    <a title="Jenn(ifer)" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/780933-jenn-ifer"><img alt="Jenn(ifer)" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1579713607p2/780933.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/399318048?book_show_action=true">Aug 24, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jenn(ifer)" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jenn(ifer)" href="/user/show/780933-jenn-ifer">Jenn(ifer)</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback " href="/book/show/889028.Wide_Sargasso_Sea">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">those who have already read Jane Eyre</span></div>

          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Jenn(ifer) by: </span>
      I would never have found Jean if not for Mariel
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/780933-jenn-ifer?shelf=summer-of-women-2012">summer-of-women-2012</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/780933-jenn-ifer?shelf=xx">xx</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/780933-jenn-ifer?shelf=read-in-2012">read-in-2012</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/780933-jenn-ifer?shelf=my-reviews-that-dont-suck">my-reviews-that-dont-suck</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer399318048" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7783328157817552391"><br/>As many of you who read my reviews are aware, I had devoted this summer to exclusively reading female writers, as my reading list was woefully lacking in books written by the fairer sex. It has been an exceptional experience for me as it has opened my eyes to such great writers as Flannery O’Connor, Virginia Woolf, Alice Munro and the incomparable Jean Rhys.<br/><br/>Jean Rhys! I feel I owe a debt to the original publishers of <i>Wide Sargasso Sea</i> because if not for its publication her exceptional early work</span>
  <span id="freeText7783328157817552391" style="display:none"><br />As many of you who read my reviews are aware, I had devoted this summer to exclusively reading female writers, as my reading list was woefully lacking in books written by the fairer sex. It has been an exceptional experience for me as it has opened my eyes to such great writers as Flannery O’Connor, Virginia Woolf, Alice Munro and the incomparable Jean Rhys.<br /><br />Jean Rhys! I feel I owe a debt to the original publishers of <i>Wide Sargasso Sea</i> because if not for its publication her exceptional early work may have remained for ever out of print and I may never have been introduced to Jean. The Jean of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/144073.Good_Morning__Midnight__Penguin_Modern_Classics_" title="Good Morning, Midnight (Penguin Modern Classics) by Jean Rhys" rel="nofollow noopener">Good Morning, Midnight</a>. The Jean of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/144069.After_Leaving_Mr__Mackenzie" title="After Leaving Mr. Mackenzie by Jean Rhys" rel="nofollow noopener">After Leaving Mr. Mackenzie</a>. She is my soul mate (if I believed in that sort of thing). We are kindred spirits.<br /><br />You see, back in the 1930’s, before the second world war when young Jean’s novels were published, they didn’t catch on with the general public. Maybe people just didn’t get her (don’t worry, Jean, I get you). They met with some critical acclaim, but widespread readership was not to be had. So with that, Jean vanished from the literary scene and spent the next twenty years living in relative obscurity. It wasn’t until the end of the 1950’s that she was located and it was discovered that she had been working on the manuscript for <i>Wide Sargasso Sea</i>. With <i>Wide Sargasso Sea</i>, Jean finally received much deserved recognition for her body of work, although with her failing health she often remarked that this recognition came ‘too late.’<br /><br />So let’s talk about <i>Wide Sargasso Sea</i>. If this had been my introduction to Jean Rhys, I don’t think I would have cared to read the rest of her work. Because, for me, this is not representative of Jean’s writing. I like the <i>idea</i> behind it. To take the frightening lunatic from <i>Jane Eyre</i> and give her a voice, tell her background, tell her story; it’s a remarkable idea. But the execution… of that I’m not convinced. It was all too heavy handed; it lacked the subtlety of her earlier work. It’s as if Jean put all of the pieces together too neatly for me and didn’t leave any room for me to have my own feelings. <br /><br />I’ll try to explain what I mean by giving you examples of two songs about loss. One is Harry Nilsson’s ‘Without You’ <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://www.youtube.com/watch?v=_bQGRRolrg0">http://www.youtube.com/watch?v=_bQGRR...</a> and the other is Glen Hansard’s ‘Leave’ <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://www.youtube.com/watch?v=TB4RlKFrw3s">http://www.youtube.com/watch?v=TB4RlK...</a>. Nilsson not only writes with beautiful simplicity, but he doesn’t put it all out there for you; he holds back just enough to keep your interest. He leaves something out, allowing the listener to fill in the blanks. There’s a subtlety to it; like secrets he hasn’t told. Wanting to know the secrets, we listen to that song again and again. That might not make sense yet, but maybe it will after you listen to the Hansard song. Yes, he writes beautifully and yes he makes you feel his loss, but damn, he sucks the air out of the room so there’s nothing left for the rest of us breathe! Stop punching me in the stomach, Glen, I get the point already. <br /><br />Damn. It pains me to write this review. I don’t want Jean tied to Glen Hansard in my memory. I want Nilsson! Thankfully, I saved <i>Quartet</i> for last. For those of you who haven’t read Jean Rhys, or those who don’t feel particularly attached to her early work, you may want to read some of the other reviews that have praised <i>WSS</i>. As for me, my heart is a little bruised today because I’m left with this taste in my mouth after finishing the final pages. It is the taste of mediocrity. But as I said in the beginning, without <i>WSS</i>, Jean would have disappeared into obscurity and who knows if her early body of work would have ever again seen the light of day. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="7783328157817552391" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_1663633786" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1663633786" />
    <a title="Roman Clodia" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/56111743-roman-clodia"><img alt="Roman Clodia" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1464021031p2/56111743.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1663633786?book_show_action=true">Jun 09, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Roman Clodia" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Roman Clodia" href="/user/show/56111743-roman-clodia">Roman Clodia</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780141182858" href="/book/show/829372.Wide_Sargasso_Sea">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/56111743-roman-clodia?shelf=contested-histories-paper">contested-histories-paper</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1663633786" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4872425453139897734">Reread for the third time 13/10/20<br/><br/>I'm blown away by this book all over again and am in awe of the magnificent way that Rhys deftly layers her art: Antoinette is both character - melancholy, traumatised by her family history, exploited, and menaced in so many ways - but she's also a figure who might stand in for a British West Indian colonial history which many people want to mute or hide away, but which still finds ways, however brutal and disturbing, to assert itself and wander the corridors of</span>
  <span id="freeText4872425453139897734" style="display:none">Reread for the third time 13/10/20<br /><br />I'm blown away by this book all over again and am in awe of the magnificent way that Rhys deftly layers her art: Antoinette is both character - melancholy, traumatised by her family history, exploited, and menaced in so many ways - but she's also a figure who might stand in for a British West Indian colonial history which many people want to mute or hide away, but which still finds ways, however brutal and disturbing, to assert itself and wander the corridors of the archetypal Victorian 'great house' of Thornfield Grange. She is described as a ghost in the third part which intersects with <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/10210.Jane_Eyre" title="Jane Eyre by Charlotte Brontë" rel="nofollow noopener">Jane Eyre</a> but is also afraid of seeing the ghost that is embodied in her own experience - this specific colonial legacy, via Antoinette, becomes a form of English haunting. <br /><br />I also noted this time round how cleverly proleptic Rhys' text is with fires and burning a repeated image, from the firing of Coulibri, the previously slave-worked sugar plantation house, to the moths which dash themselves against the candle flames recalling ideas of passion and destruction. <br /><br />Rhys is particularly adept at form: the three houses - Coulibri, the 'sweet honeymoon house' near the village of Massacre, and Thornfield echo the structural houses that trace Jane Eyre's original journey. And the use of narrative voices, shifting between Antoinette herself and Edward Rochester, is exemplary, showing us his descent into something close to incoherence as his self-fashioning narrative draws to a close, and questioning her status as 'mad' given the contrasting coherence, however confused, of her own text. <br /><br />Immensely sophisticated, that Rhys manages to say so much in 120 pages is a stunning achievement. With so much to think about, this Penguin edition with introduction, appendix, general notes and textual notes is helpful and illuminating.<br />-----------------------------------------------<br />(Original review)<br /><br />In this passionate, poetic and intense book, Jean Rhys writes back to <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/10210.Jane_Eyre" title="Jane Eyre by Charlotte Brontë" rel="nofollow noopener">Jane Eyre</a>, inverting the patterns of that book and turning `the madwoman in the attic' from silenced object of other people's discourse to a woman with a voice and subjectivity of her own.<br /><br />In three parts, this fragmentary novel gives us Antoinette's difficult childhood as a Creole (regarded as neither `white' nor `black' in post-Emancipation Jamaica), a middle narrative from Mr Rochester telling of their marriage, and a short final part from Antoinette again (now re-named by him as Bertha) from her imprisonment in the attic.<br /><br />Like Rhys' other books, this gives a voice to a traditionally muted female character but she overlays the gender concerns with an interest in a colonial legacy. The idea of the `Other' is shown to be simultaneously constructed in terms of race, gender, class and `normality' (though whether Antoinette is `mad' or not is certainly questioned by the text).<br /><br />That said, Rhys doesn't make this a predictable book and is most contrary, I think, in the central section where Rochester falls deeply under the spell of Antoinette - and it is his fear of her effect on him, perhaps, which he sees as most dangerous.<br /><br />So this is a very modern re-writing of a well-loved book, de-centreing the story of Jane Eyre and forcing us to see it anew. It is also a text which lends itself very well to postcolonial and gendered readings.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4872425453139897734" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>
